Richard Branson, Elon Musk and Other Top Execs Ask During Job Interviews","friendly_title":"What Richard Branson, Elon Musk and Other Top Execs Ask During Job Interviews","post_type":"post","available":true,"time_article":true,"comments_allowed":true,"sub_learn_more_url":"https:\/\/subscription.time.com\/storefront\/subscribe-to-time\/link\/1032487.html","show_comments":true,"short_title":"What Richard Branson, Elon Musk and Other Top Execs Ask During Job Interviews","excerpt":"\"What motivates you to get out of bed in the morning?\"","format":"article","time":{"pretty":"Dec. 2, 2015","published":"2015-12-02 15:55:53","updated":"2015-12-02 20:55:53","minute":"3:55 pm","short":"12\/2"},"authors":[{"id":13176886,"url":"http:\/\/time.com\/author\/business-insider\/","name":"Jacquelyn Smith \/ Business Insider","bio":"","short_bio":"","types":["contributor"],"twitter":"businessinsider","twitter_url":"https:\/\/twitter.com\/businessinsider","gplus":"","fb":"","email":"","thumbnail":null,"contributor":true,"search_link":false}],"has_authors":true,"content":"<p>Savvy CEOs and executives know that interview questions like, &#8220;What&#8217;s your biggest strength?&#8221; and, &#8220;What&#8217;s your biggest weakness?&#8221; aren&#8217;t as telling as they seem.<\/p>\n<p>That&#8217;s why they steer clear of these clich&eacute; queries and instead ask more meaningful ones.<\/p>\n<p>Many top execs have their one favorite go-to question that reveals everything they need to know about a job candidate.<\/p>\n<p>Here are 24 of them.<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What didn&#8217;t you get a chance to include on your r&eacute;sum&eacute;?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Billionaire Virgin Group founder Richard Branson explains in his book <em><a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/Virgin-Way-Everything-About-Leadership\/dp\/1591847370\/?tag=timecom-20\">The Virgin Way: Everything I Know About Leadership<\/a><\/em>, that he isn&#8217;t a fan of the traditional job interview, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/richard-bransons-favorite-employee-interview-question-2014-9\">reports Business Insider&#8217;s Richard Feloni<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Obviously a good CV is important, but if you were going to hire by what they say about themselves on paper, you wouldn&#8217;t need to waste time on an interview,&#8221; Branson writes. That&#8217;s why he likes to ask: What didn&#8217;t you get a chance to include on your r&eacute;sum&eacute;?<\/p>\n<div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"18\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-4724226967001-18\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1Qbx3LV\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4724226967001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div>\n<p><strong>&#8220;On a scale of one to 10, how weird are you?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>One of Zappos&#8217; core values is to &#8220;create fun and a little weirdness,&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/%20http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/henry-blodget-tony-hsieh-zappos-2010-10\">Tony Hsieh, CEO of the company, told Business Insider in 2010.<\/a><\/p>\n<p>To make sure he hires candidates with the right fit, Hsieh typically asks the question: &#8220;On a scale of one to 10, how weird are you?&#8221; He says the number isn&#8217;t too important, but it&#8217;s more about how people answer the question. Nonetheless, if &#8220;you&#8217;re a one, you probably are a little bit too straight-laced for the <a title=\"\" href=\"http:\/\/www.zappos.com\/\">Zappos<\/a> culture,&#8221; he said. &#8220;If you&#8217;re a 10, you might be too psychotic for us.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;How would you describe yourself in one word?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>The best candidates are the ones who know exactly who they are. That&#8217;s why Dara Richardson-Heron, CEO of women&#8217;s organization YWCA, always asks her candidates this question.<\/p>\n<p>Richardson-Heron has said she doesn&#8217;t judge people on the word they choose, but it does give her insight into how people package themselves. She tells <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2013\/12\/08\/business\/dara-richardson-heron-of-the-ywca-on-persistence.html\">Adam Bryant at the New York <em>Times<\/em><\/a> that she likes when people take time to ponder the question and answer thoughtfully.<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Give me an example of a time when you solved an analytically difficult problem.&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Laszlo Bock, Google&#8217;s HR boss, says the company ditched its famous brainteaser interview questions in recent years for behavioral ones.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;The interesting thing about the behavioral interview is that when you ask somebody to speak to their own experience, and you drill into that, you get two kinds of information,&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2013\/06\/20\/business\/in-head-hunting-big-data-may-not-be-such-a-big-deal.html\">Bock told the New York <em>Times<\/em><\/a>. &#8220;One is you get to see how they actually interacted in a real-world situation, and the valuable &#8216;meta&#8217; information you get about the candidate is a sense of what they consider to be difficult.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;How old were you when you had your first paying job?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Hannah Paramore, president of <a href=\"http:\/\/paramoredigital.com\/\">Paramore<\/a>, a Nashville-based interactive advertising agency, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/05\/18\/business\/corner-office-hannah-paramore-on-keeping-strengths-from-running-amok.html\">told the New York <em>Times<\/em>&#8216; Adam Bryant<\/a> that this is one of her favorite questions.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;I&#8217;m looking for how deeply instilled their work ethic and independence are versus entitlement,&#8221; she <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/2-most-important-interview-questions-2014-5\">told Business Insider<\/a>. &#8220;If they worked part time in high school and college because they needed to, especially in jobs that were just hard work, that shows a huge level of personal responsibility. I love people who have to patch success together from a number of different angles.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;&#8230; Where are you?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>According to <em><a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/Elon-Musk-SpaceX-Fantastic-Future\/dp\/0062301233\" target=\"_blank\">Elon Musk<\/a><\/em>, a new authorized biography of the SpaceX and Tesla founder written by Bloomberg Businessweek reporter Ashlee Vance, Musk likes to ask job candidates the following riddle, <a href=\"http:\/\/mashable.com\/2015\/05\/13\/elon-musk-job-interview\/#1CG80MWP0PqI\">Mashable reports<\/a>:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>You&#8217;re standing on the surface of the Earth. You walk one mile south, one mile west, and one mile north. You end up exactly where you started. Where are you?<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>The answer: you&#8217;re either at the North Pole, or somewhere close to the South Pole, Mashable reports.<\/p>\n<p>Seth Fiegerman of Mashable <a href=\"http:\/\/mashable.com\/2015\/05\/13\/elon-musk-job-interview\/#1CG80MWP0PqI\">writes<\/a>: &#8220;Musk is said to have interviewed nearly all of the first 1,000 hires at SpaceX, and many of the engineers since. He was also an active and adept recruiter at his companies, including Tesla and PayPal, the latter of which had an all-star roster of employees who went on start and fund a number of influential tech companies.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What&#8217;s your superpower &#8230; or spirit animal?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Last year writer Jeff Haden <a href=\"https:\/\/www.linkedin.com\/pulse\/article\/20140807094555-20017018-13-ceos-share-their-favorite-job-interview-questions?trk=tod-posts-post1-ptlt%3Ftrk%3Dtod-posts-post1-ptlt\">asked a bunch of smart people<\/a> from a variety of fields for their favorite interview question. <a href=\"http:\/\/www.hootsuite.com\/\">HootSuite<\/a> CEO Ryan Holmes said his is: &#8220;What&#8217;s your superpower &hellip; or spirit animal?&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;During her interview, I asked my current executive assistant what was her favorite animal. She told me it was a duck, because ducks are calm on the surface and hustling like crazy getting things done under the surface,&#8221; he told Haden. &#8220;I think this was an amazing response and a perfect description for the role of an EA. For the record, she&#8217;s been working with us for over a year now and is amazing at her job.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What is your spiritual practice?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Business Insider&#8217;s Richard Feloni <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/oprah-winfrey-asks-an-unorthodox-interview-question-2015-2\">reports<\/a> that when Oprah Winfrey was searching for a president of her television network, the Oprah Winfrey Network (OWN), &#8220;she whittled down applicants&#8221; with this question, which made some people very uncomfortable, she said <a href=\"http:\/\/www.gsb.stanford.edu\/insights\/oprah-winfrey-align-your-personality-your-purpose\">at a presentation at the Stanford Graduate School of Business last year<\/a>. &#8220;One woman even burst out crying,&#8221; Feloni reports.<\/p>\n<p>Winfrey clarified that she wasn&#8217;t asking about religion &mdash; she was asking candidates about their inner relationship with themselves.<\/p>\n<p>Winfrey said she was getting at, &#8220;What do you do for yourself? What do you do to keep yourself centered?&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/oprah-winfrey-asks-an-unorthodox-interview-question-2015-2\">Feloni writes<\/a>: &#8220;She asks this because she considers her &#8216;secret to success&#8217; the fact that she is grounded in her own self and looks for others who are as well. If we know who we are and what we want from life, Winfrey believes, then we can build meaningful relationships with others to make our visions reality.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What is your favorite quote?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Karen Davis, a senior vice president at <a href=\"http:\/\/www.hasbro.com\/en-gb\">Hasbro<\/a>, the toy and game giant, told Business Insider that because her work is focused on giving back &mdash; a big part of her job is deciding which organizations and projects Hasbro will help fund &mdash; she looks for candidates with &#8220;a true sense of passion and purpose.&#8221; And the quote question, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/curveball-interview-question-hasbro-exec-asks-2015-6\">she told Business Insider&#8217;s Rachel Sugar<\/a>, helps her figure out who applicants really are and what they truly care about.<\/p>\n<p>While there&#8217;s no &#8220;right answer,&#8221; Davis said she wants candidates who have <em>an<\/em> answer. &#8220;I want to see that somebody has been looking for sources of inspiration.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Can you tell me the story of your prior successes, challenges, and major responsibilities?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Lonne Jaffe, chief executive of software company <a href=\"http:\/\/www.syncsort.com\/en\/Home\">Syncsort<\/a>, said in a <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/07\/25\/business\/lonne-jaffe-chief-executive-of-syncsort-on-the-importance-of-setting-priorities.html\">New York <em>Times<\/em> interview<\/a> with Adam Bryant that he always wants to see how well a job candidate can tell a story.<\/p>\n<p>He <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/surprising-skill-ceo-wants-in-candidates-2014-7\">told Business Insider<\/a> that as long as we&#8217;ve had language, storytelling has been a powerful communication tool. &#8220;In business, creating a compelling narrative is invaluable for motivating a team, explaining strategic priorities in a way that&#8217;s easy for others to understand, or communicating complex ideas to customers and prospects. Successful senior-level leaders are good storytellers, and it&#8217;s also a very useful skill early on in your career.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Jaffe said he recognized the importance of storytelling early in his career while working at IBM. &#8220;Storytelling is especially important in the tech industry because technology can be very complex, and sometimes people find technical details to be somewhat boring.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"19\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-4724201670001-19\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1Qbx3LV\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4724201670001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What would you do in the event of a zombie apocalypse?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>This seems like a ridiculous question to ask, but it&#8217;s posed to every prospective employee at <a href=\"http:\/\/capriottis.com\/index.php\">Capriotti&#8217;s Sandwich Shop<\/a>, a national restaurant franchise. Ashley Morris, the company&#8217;s CEO, says it&#8217;s the best way to learn how candidates react under pressure.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;There really is no right answer, so it&#8217;s interesting to get someone&#8217;s opinion and understand how they think on their feet,&#8221; Morris <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/ridiculous-interview-question-2013-10\">explained<\/a>. &#8220;The hope is that for us, we&#8217;re going to find out who this person is on the inside and what&#8217;s really important to him, what his morals really are, and if he&#8217;ll fit on the cultural level.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Walk me through your r&eacute;sum&eacute;, particularly why you changed from one job to the next.&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>LearnVest CEO Alexa von Tobel <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2015\/04\/19\/business\/alexa-von-tobel-of-learnvest-no-really-whats-your-weakness.html\">told Adam Bryant<\/a> at the New York <em>Times<\/em> that the way a candidate responds to this question reveals their thought process, which &#8220;tells you a lot about someone.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>She said she <em>also <\/em>likes to ask about weaknesses, but if the candidate doesn&#8217;t give a real, honest answer, she&#8217;ll rephrase the question: &#8220;What are you genuinely bad at? What does your spouse or partner or the person you&#8217;re dating tell you you&#8217;re bad at? Because if they haven&#8217;t told you, then you shouldn&#8217;t be sitting here. I can&#8217;t work with you if you don&#8217;t know what you&#8217;re bad at.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;If we&#8217;re sitting here a year from now celebrating what a great 12 months it&#8217;s been for you in this role, what did we achieve together?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Randy Garutti, the CEO of <a href=\"http:\/\/www.shakeshack.com\/\">Shake Shack<\/a>, <a href=\"http:\/\/careers.geblogs.com\/13-ceos-share-their-favorite-job-interview-questions\/\">told writer Jeff Haden<\/a> that he needs to know candidates have &#8220;done their homework, truly understand our company and the role &#8230; and <em>really<\/em> want it.&#8221;<\/p><div class=\"content-ad\" id=\"content-ad-4130977\"><\/div>\n<p>Garutti continued: &#8220;The candidate should have enough strategic vision to not only talk about how good the year has been but to answer with an eye towards that bigger-picture understanding of the company &mdash; and why they want to be here.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What motivates you to get out of bed in the morning?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>In a New York <em>Times<\/em> interview with Adam Bryant, Brad Jefferson, CEO of <a href=\"http:\/\/animoto.com\/\">Animoto<\/a>, a video slide show service, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/05\/30\/business\/brad-jefferson-of-animoto-on-knowing-your-companys-heartbeat.html\">shared his three favorite interview questions<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>He especially loves this one about what motivates people because it helps him understand a candidate&#8217;s passions and what makes them tick. &#8220;I really try to get in their head about what&#8217;s going to keep them going.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Jefferson <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/3-questions-ceo-always-asks-candidates-2014-5\">told Business Insider<\/a> that it&#8217;s important to understand what motivates a person at their core because &#8220;there will always be ups and downs in any business, and you want to make sure the person will be equally motivated during difficult times, if not more so.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>He said if you &#8220;pursue something that you&#8217;re passionate about with people who motivate you, then work is really fun, even during the difficult times.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What would the closest person in your life say if I asked them, &#8216;What is the one characteristic that they totally dig about you, and the one that drives them insane?&#8217; &#8220;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Kat Cole, president of Cinnabon, told Adam Bryant in a <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/07\/20\/business\/corner-office-kat-cole-of-cinnabon-questioning-success-more-than-failure.html\">New York <em>Times<\/em> interview<\/a> that before asking questions, she likes to see how job candidates interact with people in the waiting area.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;I&#8217;ll ask people to offer the candidate a drink to see if there&#8217;s a general gratefulness there, and they&#8217;ll send me notes,&#8221; she said. &#8220;Then, when someone walks into my office, I&#8217;ll have a big wad of paper on my floor between the door and the table. I want to see if the person picks it up. I don&#8217;t make huge judgments around it, but it does give me a sense of how detail-oriented they are.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>After some conversation, she finally says: &#8220;Tell me about the closest person in your life who you&#8217;re comfortable talking about. What would they say if I asked them, &#8216;What is the one characteristic that they totally dig about you?'&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Then she&#8217;ll say: &#8220;What is the one characteristic that drives them insane, and that they would love for you to do just a little bit less?&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;People are pretty comfortable talking about that because I&#8217;ve pinpointed a person and a point of view,&#8221; she told the <em>Times<\/em>.<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;A hammer and a nail cost $1.10, and the hammer costs one dollar more than the nail. How much does the nail cost?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Jeff Zwelling, the former CEO <a href=\"http:\/\/www.convertro.com\/\">Convertro<\/a> and current COO of <a href=\"https:\/\/www.ziprecruiter.com\/\">ZipRecruiter<\/a>, told Business Insider that he often turns to tricky questions during job interviews to get a better sense of who the candidate is.<\/p>\n<p>For example, in the middle of the conversation, he often throws in this curveball math question.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Some candidates will instantly blurt out 10 cents, which is obviously wrong,&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/ceo-asks-candidates-this-trick-math-question-2014-5\">he said<\/a>. &#8220;They don&#8217;t have to get the exact right answer, which is a nickel, but I want to see them at least have a thought process behind it.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Zwelling said he understands that math isn&#8217;t everyone&#8217;s forte, but he wants them to realize that &#8220;10 cents is too easy of an answer, and that if it was that easy, I wouldn&#8217;t be asking it.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;If you worked in a restaurant, what role would you want?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p><a href=\"http:\/\/www.thoughtspot.com\/\">ThoughtSpot<\/a> CEO Ajeet Singh told Business Insider that this question gives him <em>a lot<\/em> of information about job candidates &mdash; &#8220;and he may be the only tech exec in Silicon Valley who&#8217;s asking it,&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/brilliantly-revealing-interview-question-tech-ceo-asks-2015-6\">writes Rachel Sugar<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>Singh said it&#8217;s an incredibly useful question, and not nearly as odd as it sounds.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;This question gets at the essence of what drives a person and what they like to do, what inspires them, what motivates them&#8221; he explained.&#8221;I want to see if I can get some unconventional insight into what people are like when the job search constraints are removed.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;If I were to say to a bunch of people who know you, &#8216;Give me three adjectives that best describe you,&#8217; what would I hear?'&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Michelle Peluso, CEO of Gilt Groupe, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/04\/20\/business\/michelle-peluso-of-gilt-groupe-i-dont-need-an-ivory-tower-or-an-office.html?_r=1&amp;version=meter+at+6&amp;region=FixedCenter&amp;pgtype=article&amp;priority=true&amp;module=RegiWall-Regi&amp;action=click\">told Adam Bryant of the New York <em>Times<\/em><\/a> that this question is far more telling than, &#8220;What are you good at?&#8221; which is a question she despises.<\/p>\n<p>Here&#8217;s what she tells each candidate: &#8220;OK, I&#8217;ve interviewed an eclectic crowd about you: the guy who delivers your food, the last people you worked with, the person who can&#8217;t stand you the most, your best friend from high school, your mother&#8217;s neighbor, your kindergarten teacher, your high school math teacher who loved you, and your last boss.&#8221; Then she asks: &#8220;If I were to say to them, &#8216;Give me three adjectives that best describe you,&#8217; what would I hear?&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Peluso <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/most-revealing-interview-question-2014-4\">said<\/a> if the candidate gives her three glowing adjectives, she&#8217;ll remind them that the hypothetical group includes a few people who aren&#8217;t particularly fond of them.<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Tell me something that&#8217;s true, that almost nobody agrees with you on.&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>PayPal cofounder, managing partner of the Founders Fund, and president of Clarium Capital Peter Thiel always looks to hire people who aren&#8217;t afraid to speak their minds, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.sg\/peter-thiel-loves-this-interview-question-2014-11\/\">reports Business Insider&#8217;s Aaron Taube<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>To do this, he always gives job candidates and the founders of companies seeking an investment this interview prompt: &#8220;Tell me something that&#8217;s true, that almost nobody agrees with you on.&#8221;&#8216;<\/p>\n<p>In <a href=\"http:\/\/www.forbes.com\/sites\/ryanmac\/2012\/05\/01\/reid-hoffman-and-peter-thiel-in-conversation-finding-the-best-candidates-for-the-job\/\">a 2012 interview with Forbes<\/a>, Thiel said the reason he loves this question is: &#8220;It sort of tests for originality of thinking, and to some extent, it tests for your courage in speaking up in a difficult interview context.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What was the last costume you wore?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>It doesn&#8217;t matter so much what they wore, but why they wore it. If the candidate&#8217;s reasoning matches Warby Parker&#8217;s core value of injecting &#8220;fun and quirkiness into work, life, and everything [they] do,&#8221; they might have a real shot at getting a job there.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;We find that people who are able to make the job environment fun build followership more easily,&#8221; the company&#8217;s cofounder and co-CEO <a href=\"http:\/\/qz.com\/137326\/why-your-halloween-costume-matters-if-you-want-to-work-for-warby-parker\/\">David Gilboa told <\/a><a href=\"http:\/\/qz.com\/137326\/why-your-halloween-costume-matters-if-you-want-to-work-for-warby-parker\/\">Iris Mansour at Quartz.<\/a> &#8220;If we hire the most technically skilled person in the world whose work style doesn&#8217;t fit here, they won&#8217;t be successful.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Can you tell me about a time you ran with a project from start to finish?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Jess Levin, the founder and chief executive of <a href=\"http:\/\/caratsandcake.com\/\">Carats &amp; Cake<\/a>, an online wedding resource that features curated content and information about vendors, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/ceo-shares-the-one-question-she-asks-in-every-job-interview-2015-4\">told Business Insider<\/a> she asks this because she looks for people &#8220;who get what it means to do big things without a lot of hands.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;There is no one-size-fits-all answer,&#8221; she added &mdash; but she always looks for an example that &#8220;communicates independent drive, proactive problem solving, and humility.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Can you tell me about a time when you almost gave up, how you felt about that, and what you did instead of giving up?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Wayne Jackson, chief executive of the software security firm Sonatype, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/05\/09\/business\/wayne-jackson-chief-of-sonatype-on-taking-risks.html\">told the New York <em>Times<\/em>&#8216; Adam Bryant<\/a> that in asking this question, he can learn about what people do outside of work &mdash; what drives them, what they think about, what&#8217;s important &mdash; to determine whether they have &#8220;the competitiveness and the drive to get through tough problems and tough times.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Another reason he loves this question: It helps him figure out if the candidate&#8217;s values and mindset are in line with his. &#8220;I tend to drift toward things where the stakes are relatively high, the dynamics are really complex, and teamwork matters,&#8221; he told Bryant. And it&#8217;s important that his employees do the same.<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"20\" data-continuous=\"1\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4733884396001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What would someone who doesn&#8217;t like you say about you?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Retired General Stanley McChrystal, who currently oversees the management consulting firm he founded, the <a href=\"http:\/\/mcchrystalgroup.com\/\">McChrystal Group<\/a>, told <em><a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/gp\/product\/0307465357\/?tag=timecom-20\" target=\"_blank\">The 4-Hour Workweek<\/a><\/em> author Tim Ferriss <a href=\"http:\/\/fourhourworkweek.com\/2015\/07\/05\/stanley-mcchrystal\/\">on his podcast<\/a> that this unusual interview question &#8220;puts a person in the position of having to try to articulate what they think the perception of them by others is.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>The question essentially forces candidates to consider their least attractive qualities &#8220;and also muster enough courage to share them with someone who holds power over their careers,&#8221;<a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/gen-stanley-mcchrystals-favorite-job-interview-question-2015-7\">reports Business Insider&#8217;s Richard Feloni<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;What have you invented?&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Lori Senecal, CEO of the MDC Partner Network, told Adam Bryant of the New York <em>Times<\/em> that there are three things she looks for in every job candidate, and she asks a certain set of questions to find out if they possess those traits.<\/p>\n<p class=\"story-body-text\">First, she says, she looks for the &#8220;inventor mind-set.&#8221; &#8220;I&#8217;ll say, &#8216;What have you invented?'&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/interview-questions-one-ceo-asks-every-candidate-2015-5\">Senecal told Bryant<\/a>. &#8220;That doesn&#8217;t mean you have to have created a robot that can get a beer from a fridge. It could be anything. It&#8217;s to see whether they have the mindset of creating something. That shows a desire to find fresh solutions.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p class=\"story-body-text\">Next she wants to know whether they have the ability or desire to collaborate. To figure out if they&#8217;ve got it, she says: &#8220;Talk to me about one of your greatest achievements.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p class=\"story-body-text\">And lastly, she wants to detect passion and commitment. For that, she says: &#8220;Tell me about a time when you really had to stick your neck out for the greater good of the mission.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p class=\"story-body-text\"><em>This <a Making Friends With Successful People Will Help Your Career","excerpt":"It takes a lot of energy, but the rewards will be priceless","format":"article","time":{"pretty":"Dec. 1, Entrepreneur","bio":"","short_bio":"","types":["contributor"],"twitter":"","twitter_url":"","gplus":"","fb":"","email":"","thumbnail":null,"contributor":true,"search_link":false}],"has_authors":true,"content":"<p>When you <a href=\"http:\/\/www.rich20something.com\/dont-jealous-successful-people-become-friends\/\">make friends with successful people<\/a> the sky&#8217;s the limit. This is how partnerships are made, handshake deals are done, and new audiences found. And it&#8217;s not as difficult as you might think.<\/p>\n<p>The biggest reason why connecting with people benefits you is simple: It allows you to extend your reach further, which in turn allows your ideas to make a much bigger impact.<\/p>\n<p>The result is that you achieve your goals a lot faster with a few key friends to help you out than if it is just you, banging your head against the wall.<\/p>\n<p>Additionally, the more successful people you&#8217;re connected to, <a href=\"http:\/\/lifehacker.com\/5926309\/how-the-people-around-you-affect-personal-success\" target=\"_hplink\">the more likely you are to be successful yourself<\/a> (&#8220;You&#8217;re the average of your five closest friends&#8221;).<\/p>\n<p>It&#8217;s just a good idea to get close to as many winners as possible. But this is the part where most of us throw up our hands:<\/p>\n<p><em>Duh. Connect with successful people. But how?<\/em><\/p>\n<p>Today, I&#8217;m going to teach you how to connect with <em>anybody<\/em> in the world.<\/p>\n<p><strong>Networking? Yuck.<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>I think the term is &#8220;played out&#8221; at best. At worst, it&#8217;s completely repulsive.<\/p>\n<p>Nobody wants to think of themselves as a &#8220;networker,&#8221; nobody likes to think of themselves as part of someone else&#8217;s &#8220;network,&#8221; and without question nobody enjoys &#8220;networking&#8221; mixers.<\/p>\n<p>There&#8217;s something predatory about the whole experience.<\/p>\n<p>So from here on out, we&#8217;ll frame our conversation in the context of meeting and connecting with interesting people for the simple joy of making new friends and living a better life. We&#8217;re not collecting people or trading &#8220;contacts.&#8221; We&#8217;re not dealing with baseball cards.<\/p>\n<p>These are real people (just as we are) and if you think of everyone as a human, not a potential step in your ego-driven ladder-climbing to success, I guarantee you that you will actually climb said ladder faster.<\/p>\n<p>It&#8217;s counterintuitive, but it works. I promise.<\/p>\n<div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"24\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-4105230971001-24\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1PYOfG3\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4105230971001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div>\n<p><strong>Step 1: The Internet cocktail party &mdash; how to find people with mutual interests and make them love you<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Do you ever suspect successful people all know each other? Like an elite secret society?<\/p>\n<p>Well, you&#8217;re partially right. But you can get an invite to the club (meetings are in a basement in Southeastern Utah, by the way).<\/p>\n<p>When I first started my blog <a href=\"http:\/\/www.rich20something.com\/blog\/\">Rich20Something<\/a>, I didn&#8217;t know anybody. It was like walking into a cocktail party by myself where everyone was already acquainted, and not knowing a single soul.<\/p>\n<p>In that situation, the only logical thing you can do is start introducing yourself to people in a way that prompts them to respond to you.<\/p>\n<p>You don&#8217;t want to be the only one babysitting a drink in the corner, do you?<\/p>\n<p>I didn&#8217;t have much to give at the time, but I did have my little website. So I decided to conduct interviews. Why interviews? Well, let&#8217;s face it: Everyone likes to talk about themselves &mdash; and anyone who&#8217;s been around the Internet block a few times knows that having your name in as many places as possible is helpful. It all adds up.<\/p>\n<p>So I just searched on Twitter, Facebook, etcetera to find people with sizable audiences who seemed like they had something interesting to say. I wrote literally <em>dozens<\/em> of emails to different bloggers and entrepreneurs asking the simple question, &#8220;Can I interview you?&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>Looking back on it, my approach was juvenile and ugly. But it got the job done. I&#8217;ll remind you over and over again, <a href=\"http:\/\/www.entrepreneur.com\/article\/246399\">it&#8217;s more important to just do it than to get it 100 perfect right.<\/a><\/p>\n<p>I personalized each message, pressed send, and prayed. Soon I started getting actual responses&#8211;mind blown. Then I created a page of interviews &#8212; which ended up being the first page of this website. In one fell swoop I&#8217;d created some cool content for my readers (which at that point, was basically just my mom) and I&#8217;d made some connections.<\/p>\n<p>People began to know who I am. I was no longer a complete stranger at the party.<\/p>\n<p>If I were doing this entire exercise over again, I would keep it super short and distill the information into only the essential points. Most people aren&#8217;t jerks &#8211; so they&#8217;d like to be able to read your stuff.<\/p>\n<p>But <em>everyone<\/em> is busy and overwhelmed. So make it easy on them. Use this template:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p><em>Hey (name), My name is Daniel and I do ________ (insert job, blog, etc). I saw your work on _______ and really enjoyed it. I&#8217;m just reaching out because __________ (insert common interest, solution to their problem, etc). Do you have 5 minutes to chat any time in the next 2 weeks? I have some time on __________ (name a few specific days\/times) Thanks! Daniel<\/em><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>If you do this 100 times and make 10 new friends, that alone is completely worth the effort. However, as you practice more and more, you&#8217;ll start refining your process and most likely, your response rates will increase.<\/p><div class=\"content-ad\" id=\"content-ad-4131216\"><\/div>\n<p>I got to a point where nine of 10 people were responding to me. That&#8217;s when I knew I was onto something.<\/p>\n<p><em>&#8220;What if I don&#8217;t have a blog?&#8221;<\/em><\/p>\n<p>Get one! <a href=\"http:\/\/www.rich20something.com\/want-build-audience-dont-start-blog-instead\/\" target=\"_hplink\">Building an audience<\/a> is one of the most powerful things you can do with your time.<\/p>\n<p>But if you&#8217;re not there yet, you need to find something that adds <em>value<\/em> to the person&#8217;s life.<\/p>\n<p>Do some research to find out some of their passions, hobbies or pain points, then send them related content. Are they looking for the solution to a particular problem that you can help them with? (<span style=\"text-decoration:underline;\">Hint<\/span>: following them on Twitter often reveals those little annoyances that you can fix. Twitter is where people go to complain.)<\/p>\n<p>Basically, be interesting and relevant. It&#8217;s not as hard as it sounds to find common interests, connections or ways to help someone.<\/p>\n<p><em>Something to remember: <\/em>You have to choose the <em>right<\/em> time to reach out to someone. Just because you got your hands on the email address of a New York <em>Times<\/em> bestselling author doesn&#8217;t mean it&#8217;s the right time to connect with them.<\/p>\n<p>Do your research. I&#8217;ll frequently get the contact information of people I&#8217;d like to connect with but hold off on getting in touch. It&#8217;s not the right time if I know when we talk, I won&#8217;t have anything particularly interesting to say.<\/p>\n<p>Again, we&#8217;re not just collecting people here, we&#8217;re making friends. <em>Be a good friend.<\/em><\/p>\n<p><strong>Step 2: Ride the value wave<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>I almost hate using the world &#8220;value&#8221; so many times, but it does a pretty good job of describing what you should be giving to your new friends. &#8220;Value&#8221; can mean many things &mdash; from a physical gift, to an important connection, to simply a warm feeling.<\/p>\n<p>There&#8217;s always something to contribute.<\/p>\n<p>That being said, now that you&#8217;ve started that friendship, it&#8217;s time to give, give, give. Really start digging deep and think about how you can be of service to people. But the key is to think of the relationship as a &#8220;long game.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>I want you to completely forget about what the other person can do for you. At least for now. For the time being, it&#8217;s all about them.<\/p>\n<p>Many people, myself included, make the mistake of thinking that as soon as you&#8217;ve made a connection with someone and offered a sliver of value, you can immediately extract value from them by asking for a favor, connection, etc.<\/p>\n<p><em>So wrong!<\/em> In fact, think of this whole value process as depositing money into a savings account. All the things you do for the other person is the principal. That&#8217;s not to be touched.<\/p>\n<p>You can only start &#8220;withdrawing&#8221; value from the relationship once you&#8217;ve contributed enough to generate some interest. Trust me, I learned this the hard way &#8211; often bumping my head and wondering why &#8211; as I asked important people I&#8217;d <em>just<\/em> met to immediately do something for me that was equal or greater value to what I&#8217;d just done for them.<\/p>\n<p>Don&#8217;t be <em>that<\/em> guy (or gal).<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"25\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-4443572678001-25\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1PYOfG3\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4443572678001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/span><\/p>\n<p><strong>Step 3: Reap the rewards of real friendships<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Now that you&#8217;ve become friends with and demonstrated your value to whatever organization\/blogger\/entrepreneur, you are officially part of the team. You&#8217;re someone who can be relied upon. Now you can take on <em>more<\/em> responsibility to help shape the brand and direction.<\/p>\n<p>Here&#8217;s what I did: I leveraged my new credibility to reach out to more influencers! With the support of organizations I believed in and media outlets I subscribed to, I asked my new friends if they&#8217;d be interested in doing a guest post on <a href=\"http:\/\/www.rich20something.com\/blog\/\">Rich20Something.<\/a><\/p>\n<p>Does this entire process take a lot of energy?<\/p>\n<p><em>Yes.<\/em> But the rewards are quite literally <em>priceless<\/em>. It&#8217;s not about connecting with <em>specific<\/em> people. It&#8217;s about the <em>process<\/em> of identifying individuals you want to meet, becoming great friends with them, and seeing where things go.<\/p>\n<p><em>Now imagine doing this for decades<\/em>. You could go anywhere you want, have access to almost anything you desire and know that in an instant, you&#8217;d have the resources to solve your toughest problems. Your friends become your biggest assets.<\/p>\n<p><em>This <a href=\"http:\/\/www.entrepreneur.com\/article\/251885\">article<\/a> originally appeared on <a href=\"http:\/\/www.entrepreneur.com\">Entrepreneur.com<\/a><\/em><\/p>\n<p><em>More from Entrepreneur.com:<\/em><\/p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"http:\/\/www.entrepreneur.com\/article\/248304\" Steve Jobs Masterfully Handled Criticism","excerpt":"\"Mistakes will be made\"","format":"article","time":{"pretty":"Nov. 24, 2015","published":"2015-11-24 17:59:27","updated":"2015-11-24 22:59:27","minute":"5:59 pm","short":"11\/24"},"authors":[{"id":13176886,"url":"http:\/\/time.com\/author\/business-insider\/","name":"Biz Carson \/ Business Insider","bio":"","short_bio":"","types":["contributor"],"twitter":"businessinsider","twitter_url":"https:\/\/twitter.com\/businessinsider","gplus":"","fb":"","email":"","thumbnail":null,"contributor":true,"search_link":false}],"has_authors":true,"content":"<p>It&#8217;s 1997, and Steve Jobs has just returned to the company he was fired from 12 years before.<\/p>\n<p>On stage at Apple&#8217;s World Wide Developers Conference, Jobs was holding a rare Q&amp;A with developers when one audience member stood up and lobbed an insult at Jobs.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Mr. Jobs, you&#8217;re a bright and influential man,&#8221; he starts out with a flat tone. The audience laughs in the pause.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Here it comes,&#8221; Job responds with a smile.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;It&#8217;s sad and clear that on several counts you&#8217;ve discussed, you don&#8217;t know what you&#8217;re talking about. [Audience laughter]. I would like, for example, for you to express in clear terms how, say, Java and any of its incarnations addresses the ideas embodied in OpenDoc. And when you&#8217;re finished with that, perhaps you can tell us what you personally have been doing for the last seven years,&#8221; he says.<\/p>\n<p>At that point, the audience fell quiet and someone is heard saying &#8220;Ouch.&#8221; (Jobs had spent the last seven years not at Apple.)<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"28\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-4443572678001-28\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1PMOkwc\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"4443572678001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/span><\/p>\n<p><strong>&#8220;Mistakes will be made&#8221;<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Jobs&#8217; response in the five minutes that follows is a masterclass in how to gracefully turn an insult into an impromptu speech on vision.<\/p>\n<p>First, Jobs politely responds.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;You know, you can please some of the people some of the time, but&hellip;,&#8221; Jobs paused. &#8220;One of the hardest things when you&rsquo;re trying to effect change is that people like this gentleman are right in some areas.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>He acknowledges that there are things OpenDoc does that he&#8217;s not even familiar with, but that doesn&#8217;t stop him in planning Apple&#8217;s future.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;The hardest thing is: how does that fit in to a cohesive, larger vision, that&rsquo;s going to allow you to sell 8 billion dollars, 10 billion dollars of product a year? And, one of the things I&rsquo;ve always found is that you&rsquo;ve got to start with the customer experience and work backwards for the technology.&rdquo; You can&rsquo;t start with the technology and try to figure out where you&rsquo;re going to try to sell it. And I made this mistake probably more than anybody else in this room. And I got the scar tissue to prove it. And I know that it&rsquo;s the case,&#8221; Jobs said.<\/p><div class=\"content-ad\" id=\"content-ad-4126440\"><\/div>\n<p>&#8220;And as we have tried to come up with a strategy and a vision for Apple, it started with &#8216;What incredible benefits can we give to the customer? Where can we take the customer?&#8217; Not starting with &#8216;Let&rsquo;s sit down with the engineers and figure out what awesome technology we have and then how are we going to market that?&#8217; And I think that&rsquo;s the right path to take,&#8221; Jobs continued.<\/p>\n<p>He apologizes for killing off some of the software, but passionately stands by his employees who were working hard to get Apple back to the place where he could hold up a box and people would go &#8220;Whoa! Yes!&#8221; and want it. (Think about what Apple events are like today.)<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Mistakes will be made, some people will be pissed off, some people will not know what they&rsquo;re talking about, but I think it&rsquo;s so much better than where things were not very long ago. And I think we&rsquo;re going to get there,&#8221; Jobs concludes.<\/p>\n<div class=\"article-video\">\n<div class=\"time-embed time-embed__youtube video-wrapper\" data-provider=\"youtube\" data-url=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v=FF-tKLISfPE\" >\n<div class=\"embed-youtube\"><iframe width=\"500\" height=\"375\" src=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/embed\/FF-tKLISfPE?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen><\/iframe><\/div>\n<\/div>\n<\/div>\n<p><em>This <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/steve-jobs-reaction-to-insult-2015-10\">article<\/a> originally appeared That is very uncommon in American business. I read and think. So I do more reading and thinking, and make less impulse decisions than most people in business. I do it because I like this kind of life.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><em>Source: <\/em><a href=\"http:\/\/www.wiley.com\/WileyCDA\/Section\/id-817935.html\">Wiley<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><strong>Always know who you&#8217;re dealing with<\/strong><\/p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;You can&#8217;t make a good deal with a bad person.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><em>Source: <a href=\"http:\/\/www.forbes.com\/sites\/erikaandersen\/2013\/12\/02\/23-quotes-from-warren-buffett-on-life-and-generosity\/\">Forbes<\/a><\/em><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><strong>Act with integrity<\/strong><\/p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;It takes 20 years to build a reputation and five minutes to ruin it. pm","short":"11\/20"},"authors":[{"id":13176886,"url":"http:\/\/time.com\/author\/business-insider\/","name":"Will Martin \/ Business Insider","bio":"","short_bio":"","types":["contributor"],"twitter":"businessinsider","twitter_url":"https:\/\/twitter.com\/businessinsider","gplus":"","fb":"","email":"","thumbnail":null,"contributor":true,"search_link":false}],"has_authors":true,"content":"<p>Whether it&#8217;s going to the gym, reading the paper, or enjoying a cup of coffee, most of us have things in our morning routine that we rarely skip.<\/p>\n<p>Getting into a good morning routine can help us be more productive throughout the day. The consistency can also be comforting.<\/p>\n<p>With the help of this <a href=\"https:\/\/www.sleepypeople.com\/blog\/how-successful-people-start-their-day\">great infographic from sleepypeople.com<\/a>, we decided to look at the morning routines of some of the world&#8217;s most successful business people and politicians.<\/p>\n<p><strong>U.S. President Barack Obama hits the gym <em>every<\/em> morning<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>The President of the United States and arguably the world&rsquo;s most powerful man, Obama isn&rsquo;t one to hang around in the mornings.<\/p>\n<p>Sleepypeople.com tells us that the president is up by 6:45 a.m. every morning. Then, he goes to gym to do both weights and cardio.<\/p>\n<p>Once he&rsquo;s finished pumping iron, Obama will head back to eat breakfast with his wife Michelle, and his two daughters Malia and Sasha, before sending the girls off to school.<\/p>\n<p>After the kids are gone, it&#8217;s time to get down to the serious business of running the country.<\/p>\n<p><strong>British Prime Minister David Cameron avoids watching TV in the morning<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Earlier this year, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v=z9hqE5HVVQk\">a video from the Sun<\/a> showed that British Prime Minister David Cameron starts his day at 6:00 a.m, and will go through important government business until around 8:00 a.m, when he joins his wife Samantha, and kids Arthur, Nancy, and Florence for a bite of breakfast.<\/p>\n<p>Cameron&rsquo;s private flat in No.10 Downing Street has a strict no TV rule in the mornings, and in the past the Prime Minister has said that he thinks his children should be &ldquo;doing something&rdquo; in the morning before school.<\/p>\n<p>Cameron is also an avid follower of media coverage and will take time to catch up on the news every morning.<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"36\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-115568863001-36\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1kLmPGE\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"115568863001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/p>\n<p><strong>Former British Prime Minister Winston Churchill wasn&#8217;t really a morning person<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Another Conservative Prime Minister of Britain, albeit of a slightly different era. According to sleepypeople.com, Churchill woke up at 7:30 a.m. every morning. Pretty normal right? Not when you consider that he didn&rsquo;t usually actually get out of bed until 11:00 a.m.<\/p>\n<p>What did he spend those three and a half hours doing, you ask? Churchill would eat breakfast and read the newspapers (no smartphones back then), as well as dictating correspondence to his numerous secretaries.<\/p>\n<p><strong>Starbucks CEO Howard Schultz favors a morning cup of joe<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Perhaps unsurprisingly for the man in charge of Starbucks, Howard Schultz&rsquo;s morning routine involves coffee.<\/p>\n<p>In a 2012 <a href=\"http:\/\/www.bloomberg.com\/bw\/articles\/2012-04-12\/how-to-make-coffee-at-home-howard-schultz\">interview with Bloomberg BusinessWeek<\/a>, Schultz talked about how he gets up at around 4:30 a.m. every morning to walk his three dogs, before coming home and making coffee for himself and his wife by 5:45 a.m.<\/p>\n<p>Schlutz is pretty particular about the kind of coffee he drinks, using a &ldquo;coarse grind of aged Sumatra&rdquo; steeped for 3-4 mins in boiling water, according to BusinessWeek. After that, it&rsquo;s time for work.<\/p><div class=\"content-ad\" id=\"content-ad-4122662\"><\/div>\n<p><strong>Former Apple CEO Steve Jobs gave himself a motivational speech every morning<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>The late Steve Jobs, famous for his all black clothing, turtlenecks, and for turning Apple into the world&rsquo;s biggest company would do one simple thing every morning to ensure that he got the best out of himself.<\/p>\n<p>After waking up, sleepypeople.com says Jobs would go to the mirror, look at himself and ask: &ldquo;If today was the last day of my life, would I be happy with what I&rsquo;m about to do today?&rdquo;<\/p>\n<p>If Jobs found himself saying no to this question too often, he said he knew that something needed to change.<\/p>\n<p><strong>Microsoft co-founder Bill Gates likes to train mind and body<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Another tech billionaire Bill Gates, the cofounder of Microsoft and one of the world&rsquo;s richest men, likes to exercise both mind and body first thing in the morning.<\/p>\n<p>Sleepypeople.com says that Gates spends an hour on the treadmill doing cardio exercise, but watches instructional videos from the Teaching Company while doing so, ensuring that all parts of his health are looked after.<\/p>\n<p><strong>Facebook creator Mark Zuckerberg often skips bedtime altogether<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>The 31-year-old Harvard dropout and founder of Facebook is well known for almost always wearing a plain gray T-shirt, saying in a 2014 interview that wearing the same shirt helps allow him <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/mark-zuckerberg-same-t-shirt-2014-11\">&#8220;to make as few decisions as possible.&#8221;<\/a><\/p>\n<p>So presumably, he gets up, goes to his closet and picks his favorite gray T-shirt and goes to work.<\/p>\n<p>According to sleepypeople.com, Zuckerberg often doesn&rsquo;t sleep very much, and he has been known to stay up until 6 a.m. talking with Facebook employees.<\/p>\n<div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"37\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-711047870001-37\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"711047870001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div>\n<p><strong>Amazon&#8217;s Jeff Bezos likes to eat a leisurely breakfast<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Unlike lots of the world&rsquo;s most successful people, Jeff Bezos, the founder of e-commerce giant Amazon, is not a morning person.<\/p>\n<p>Bezos places a lot of emphasis on getting a good night&rsquo;s sleep. In 1999 he <a href=\"http:\/\/www.wsj.com\/news\/articles\/SB923008887262090895\">told the Wall Street <em>Journal<\/em><\/a> that he feels &ldquo;so much better all day long if I&rsquo;ve had eight hours.&rdquo;<\/p>\n<p>He has also previously spoken of deliberately avoiding early morning meetings so that he can spend his mornings having a leisurely breakfast with his wife Mackenzie and their four children.<\/p>\n<p>According to sleepypeople.com, avoiding super early mornings allows Bezos to get away without using an alarm clock, and get a natural night&rsquo;s sleep. He also gives himself time to eat a healthy breakfast, rather than cramming down fatty convenience foods.<\/p>\n<p><em>This <a Buffett &mdash; <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/warren-buffett-career-advice-2014-10\">now worth more than $71 billion<\/a> &mdash; has had a hunger for wealth since he was a tube-socked teenager.<\/p>\n<p>Through numerous schemes, the would-be Oracle of Omaha amassed the equivalent of $53,000 by the time he was 16, enough money that <a href=\"http:\/\/www.newrepublic.com\/article\/books-and-arts\/the-master-money\" target=\"_blank\">he nearly refused his father&#8217;s request to go to college, because he didn&#8217;t see the point.<\/a><\/p>\n<p>By looking through Alice Schroeder&#8217;s biography, <em><a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/The-Snowball-Warren-Buffett-Business\/dp\/0553384619?tag=timecom-20\">The Snowball: Warren Buffett and the Business of Life<\/a>, <\/em>we can see that Buffett has always had a gift for manipulating money &mdash; and people.<\/p>\n<p>Here are a half-dozen of his early hustles.<\/p>\n<p><strong>He delivered The Washington <em>Post<\/em><\/strong><\/p>\n<p>Buffett&#8217;s father, Howard, was elected to the U.S. House of Representatives when Buffett was a teen, and the family had to move from Omaha to the nation&#8217;s capital.<\/p>\n<p>As Schroeder notes, the young Buffett immediately set to work making money with the most traditional of hustles &mdash; dutifully delivering newspapers. But by handing out the <em>Post<\/em>, Buffett was making more money than most grown-ups.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;Just from pitching newspapers a couple hours a day, he was earning $175 a month, more money than his teachers,&#8221; she writes.<\/p>\n<p>He also sold calendars to his newspaper clients, bringing in a little extra.<\/p>\n<p><strong>He sold used golf balls<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>If you wanted to get a golf ball on the cheap back in the 1940s, you could do worse than buying Buffett&#8217;s at $6 for a dozen.<\/p>\n<p>Buffett&#8217;s friends and family thought he scooped the balls out of water traps, but the young entrepreneur got them by ordering from a provider in Chicago.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;They were classy balls,&#8221; <a href=\"http:\/\/books.google.com\/books?id=tU_CAUXWpCsC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=snowball+buffett&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=lZpiVKO1K-XnsAS85YC4Aw&amp;ved=0CCgQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=sawyer&amp;f=false\" target=\"_blank\">Buffett told Schroeder<\/a>. &#8220;Titleist and Spalding Dots and Maxlis, which I bought for three and a half bucks a dozen. They looked brand new. He probably got them the way we first tried to get them, out of water traps, only he was better.&#8221;<\/p><div class=\"content-ad\" id=\"content-ad-4106414\"><\/div>\n<p><strong>He sold stamps<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>If you needed a fancy stamp, you could turn to Buffett&#8217;s Approval Service, which sold collectible stamps to collectors around the country.<\/p>\n<p class=\"p1\"><span class=\"s1\"><div class=\"article-video\"><div class=\"video-wrapper brightcove-size-large\" data-pos=\"40\" data-continuous=\"1\" data-playlist=\"0\"><object id=\"bc-video-3945710538001-40\" class=\"BrightcoveExperience\"><param name=\"bgcolor\" value=\"#000000\"><param name=\"width\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"height\" value=\"100%\"><param name=\"playerID\" value=\"1847503743001\"><param name=\"isVid\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"isUI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"dynamicStreaming\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"videoSmoothing\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"linkBaseURL\" value=\"http:\/\/ti.me\/1SFW7u7\"><param name=\"includeAPI\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"templateLoadHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateLoad\"><param name=\"templateReadyHandler\" value=\"Time.application.brightcoveFacade._onTemplateReady\"><param name=\"wmode\" value=\"opaque\"><param name=\"htmlFallback\" value=\"true\"><param name=\"@videoPlayer\" value=\"3945710538001\"><\/object><\/div><\/div><\/span><\/p>\n<p><strong>He buffed cars<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>The teenage Buffett partnered with his friend Lou Battistone to form Buffett&#8217;s Showroom Shine. The car-buffing business ran out of Battistone&#8217;s dad&#8217;s used car parking lot &mdash; though Schroeder <a href=\"http:\/\/books.google.com\/books?id=tU_CAUXWpCsC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=snowball+buffett&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=lZpiVKO1K-XnsAS85YC4Aw&amp;ved=0CCgQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=sawyer&amp;f=false\" target=\"_blank\">reports<\/a> that the duo abandoned the business when it turned out to be too much manual labor.<\/p>\n<p><strong>He set up a pinball machine business<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>When Buffett was 17 he had his biggest money-making idea: pinball.<\/p>\n<p>He made <a href=\"http:\/\/books.google.com\/books?id=tU_CAUXWpCsC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=snowball+buffett&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=lZpiVKO1K-XnsAS85YC4Aw&amp;ved=0CCgQ6AEwAA#v=snippet&amp;q=I%20bought%20this%20old%20pinball%20machine&amp;f=false\" target=\"_blank\">the following pitch to his friend Don Danley<\/a>:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>I bought this old pinball machine for 25 bucks, and we can have a partnership. Your part of the deal is to fix it up. And look it, we&#8217;ll tell Frank Erico, the barber, &#8216;We represent Wilson&#8217;s Coin-Operated Machine Company, and we have a proposition from Mr. Wilson. It&#8217;s at no risk to you. Let&#8217;s put this nickel machine in the back, Mr. Erico, and your customers can play while they wait. And we&#8217;ll split the money.&#8217;<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>The pinball machine was a hit: Buffett counted $4 in nickels on the first evening. The pair soon set up pinballs in barbershops all over Washington.<\/p>\n<p><strong>And he turned the horse track into a very lucrative playground<\/strong><\/p>\n<p>When still in Omaha, the young Buffett found a bull market in the Ak-Sar-Ben arena, a horse-racing track that operated from 1919 to 1995.<\/p>\n<p>He and a friend would go to the race track, and though the duo was too young to make bets, Buffett quickly found a way to make money: by <em>stooping<\/em>, which was like <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/dumpster-diving-in-brooklyn-photos-2014-10\" target=\"_blank\">dumpster diving<\/a> for race track tickets.<\/p>\n<p>Here&#8217;s Buffett&#8217;s <a href=\"http:\/\/books.google.com\/books?id=tU_CAUXWpCsC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=snowball+buffett&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=lZpiVKO1K-XnsAS85YC4Aw&amp;ved=0CCgQ6AEwAA#v=snippet&amp;q=I%20bought%20this%20old%20pinball%20machine&amp;f=false\" target=\"_blank\">description<\/a>:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>At the start of racing season you get all these people who&#8217;d never seen a race except in the movies. And they&#8217;d think that if your horse came in second or third, you didn&#8217;t get paid, because all the emphasis is on the winner, so they&#8217;d throw away [second-] and [third-place] tickets.<\/p>\n<p>The other time you would hit it big was when there was a disputed race. That little light would go on that said &#8216;contested&#8217; or &#8216;protest.&#8217; By that time, some people had thrown away their tickets. Meanwhile, we were just gobbling them up.<\/p>\n<p>It was awful; people would spit on the floor. But we had great fun.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>And if the boys found any winning tickets, Buffett&#8217;s aunt Alice would cash them in for the boys.<\/p>\n<p>Buffett went a step further: using his love of math and of collecting information, he and a friend put together a tip sheet for bettors at the race track. Soon they were out hawking &#8220;Stable Boy Selections,&#8221; a tip sheet that the boys typed out on an old Royal typewriter in Buffett&#8217;s basement.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;We were in the track, yelling, &#8216;Get your Stable-Boy Selections!'&#8221; Buffett tells Schroeder. &#8220;At 25 cents, we were a cut-rate product. They shut down Stable-Boy selections fast because they were getting a cut on everything sold in the place except for us.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/steve-jobs-richard-branson-billionaires-big-break-2014-8?op=1\">Like other self-made billionaires<\/a>, Buffett started early.<\/p>\n<p><em>This <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/how-a-young-warren-buffett-made-money-2014-11\">article<\/a> originally appeared on <a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\">Business Insider<\/a><\/em><\/p>\n<p><em>More from Business Insider:<\/em><\/p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/how-rich-warren-buffett-was-at-your-age-2015-8\">How rich Warren Buffett
